In reply to this message
Preventing cheaters in Fog Of War Games
tl;dr: I think we can use blockchains to solve cheating in Fog of War games.

Can Fog of War be solved for decentralised blockchain games? I think so! Moreover, the nature how blockchains work make them uniquely suited to solving this problem.

edward-thomson.medium.com/preventing-cheaters-in-fog-of-war-games-69f202fbe107
4*
13:13
42 üöß *OpenAI
In reply to this message
Fog of War is a concept that refers to the uncertainty or lack of situational awareness experienced by participants in military operations or games. It is often implemented in real-time strategy games, where only a portion of the map is initially revealed to players, and the rest becomes visible as they explore it with their units. Fog of War can be exploited by players who use cheats or hacks to see hidden parts of the map or the positions of their opponents' units.

Private Set Intersection (PSI) is a cryptographic technique that can be used to solve the Fog of War problem in decentralized games on the blockchain. PSI allows two parties to compute the intersection of their respective sets of data without revealing the actual contents of the sets to each other. This can be used to reveal only the necessary information to each player in a game, while keeping the rest hidden.

However, it is important to note that while PSI can help solve the Fog of War problem in decentralized games, it is not a robust solution against dishonest players who may try to lie about their positions or manipulate the game in some other way. To ensure fairness in decentralized games, additional measures such as game audits, randomized sampling, and game validators may be necessary.
I
13:14
Ilyar
In reply to this message
How does Private Set Intersection work, give an example?
4*
13:15
42 üöß *OpenAI
In reply to this message
Private Set Intersection (PSI) is a cryptographic technique that allows two parties to compute the intersection of their respective sets of data without revealing the actual contents of the sets to each other. Here's an example to illustrate how it works:

Imagine that Alice and Bob are playing a decentralized game on the blockchain where each has a set of data that represents the positions of their units on the game map. Alice's set includes the positions (1, 2), (3, 4), and (5, 6), while Bob's set includes the positions (2, 3), (4, 5), and (6, 7).

To compute the intersection of their sets using PSI, Alice and Bob follow these steps:

1. Alice and Bob both generate a random number and use it to create a hash of each element in their respective sets. For example, Alice might create the hashes h1 = hash(1, 2), h2 = hash(3, 4), and h3 = hash(5, 6), while Bob creates the hashes h4 = hash(2, 3), h5 = hash(4, 5), and h6 = hash(6, 7).

2. Alice and Bob exchange the hashes of their sets, but not the actual elements. In this case, Alice would send Bob the hashes h1, h2, and h3, and Bob would send Alice the hashes h4, h5, and h6.

3. Alice and Bob compare the hashes they received from each other. If any of the hashes match, it means that the corresponding element exists in both sets, and thus is part of the intersection. In this case, Alice would see that h4 matches h1, which means that the position (2, 3) exists in both sets.

4. Alice and Bob can repeat this process multiple times with different random numbers to increase the security of the intersection.

By using PSI, Alice and Bob can compute the intersection of their sets without revealing the actual elements of the sets to each other. This allows them to reveal only the necessary information to each other in the game, while keeping the rest hidden.

42 üöß *OpenAI
In reply to this message
Here is a script in Python that demonstrates how Private Set Intersection (PSI) works using the example from the previous answer:

import hashlib

# Alice's set of data
alice_set = [(1, 2), (3, 4), (5, 6)]

# Bob's set of data
bob_set = [(2, 3), (4, 5), (6, 7)]

# Alice's hash function
def hash_alice(data):
# Use SHA-256 as the hash function
h = hashlib.sha256()
h.update(str(data).encode())
return h.hexdigest()

# Bob's hash function
def hash_bob(data):
# Use SHA-256 as the hash function
h = hashlib.sha256()
h.update(str(data).encode())
return h.hexdigest()

# Generate a random number for Alice and Bob
alice_random = 12345
bob_random = 67890

# Hash each element in Alice's set using Alice's hash function and the random number
alice_hashes = [hash_alice((data, alice_random)) for data in alice_set]

# Hash each element in Bob's set using Bob's hash function and the random number
bob_hashes = [hash_bob((data, bob_random)) for data in bob_set]

# Exchange the hashes
received_alice = bob_hashes
received_bob = alice_hashes

# Compute the intersection by comparing the received hashes
intersection = []
for h1 in received_alice:
for h2 in received_bob:
if h1 == h2:
intersection.append(h1)

print(f"Intersection: {intersection}")

In this script, Alice and Bob each have a set of data and a hash function that takes an element of the set and a random number as input and returns a hash. They exchange the hashes of their sets and compare them to find the intersection.

Running this script should print the intersection of Alice's and Bob's sets, which in this case should be an empty list because there are no elements in common between the two sets.

You can try changing the elements in the sets and the random numbers to see how the intersection changes. You can also try using different hash functions or increasing the number of elements in the sets to see how the performance of the script changes.

h.update(str(data).encode())

You are correct that in order to implement the Fog of War in a decentralized game using Private Set Intersection (PSI), Alice and Bob must use different random numbers. This is because the purpose of using different random numbers is to prevent each party from learning the actual elements of the other party's set.

To illustrate how this can be done in the context of a decentralized game, let's consider a modified version of the example used in the previous answers. In this modified example, Alice and Bob are playing a decentralized game on the blockchain where each has a set of data that represents the positions of their units on the game map. Alice's set includes the positions (1, 2), (3, 4), and (5, 6), while Bob's set includes the positions (2, 3), (4, 5), and (6, 7).

To compute the intersection of their sets using PSI, Alice and Bob follow these steps:

1. Alice and Bob both generate a random number and use it to create a hash of each element in their respective sets. For example, Alice might create the hashes h1 = hash_alice((1, 2), alice_random), h2 = hash_alice((3, 4), alice_random), and h3 = hash_alice((5, 6), alice_random), while Bob creates the hashes h4 = hash_bob((2, 3), bob_random), h5 = hash_bob((4, 5), bob_random), and h6 = hash_bob((6, 7), bob_random).

2. Alice and Bob exchange the hashes of their sets, but not the actual elements. In this case, Alice would send Bob the hashes h1, h2, and h3, and Bob would send Alice the hashes h4, h5, and h6.

3. Alice and Bob compare the hashes they received from each other. If any of the hashes match, it means that the corresponding element exists in both sets, and thus is part of the intersection. In this case, Alice would see that h4 matches h1, which means that the position (2, 3) exists in both sets.

4. Alice and Bob can repeat this process multiple times with different random numbers to increase the security of the intersection.

By using PSI, Alice and Bob can compute the intersection of their sets without revealing the actual elements of the sets to each other. This allows them to reveal only the necessary information to each other in the game, while keeping the rest hidden.

To prevent Bob from learning Alice's random number, Alice and Bob can use additional cryptographic techniques such as multiparty computation or homomorphic encryption. These techniques allow Alice and Bob to compute the intersection of their sets without revealing the actual elements or the random numbers to each other.

For example, Alice and Bob can use a multiparty computation protocol such as Secure Multiparty Computation (SMC) to compute the intersection without revealing their sets or random numbers to each other. In this case, Alice and Bob would need to use a third party, such as a trusted server or a smart contract, to facilitate the computation of the intersection.

Alternatively, Alice and Bob can use homomorphic encryption to encrypt their sets and random numbers before exchanging them, and then decrypt the results of the intersection after it is computed. This allows them to compute the intersection without revealing the actual elements or the random numbers to each other.

It is important to note that implementing these techniques can be technically challenging and may require a deep understanding of cryptography.

You are correct, the nonce variable is not defined in the script. In homomorphic encryption, the nonce is a unique value that is used to ensure the security of the encrypted data. It is important to use a different nonce for each encryption operation to prevent attacks such as replay attacks.

In reply to this message 
const nacl = require('tweetnacl')

function toHex(buf) {
  return Buffer.from(buf).toString('hex')
}

function toBuffer(hex) {
  return Uint8Array.from(Buffer.from(hex, 'hex'))
}

// Encrypt the sets using the public keys of the other party
function encrypted(set, secretKey, otherPartyPK, nonce) {
  const enc = new TextEncoder()
  return set.map((item) =>
    toHex(nacl.box(enc.encode(item), nonce, otherPartyPK, secretKey))
  )
}

function PSI(secretKey, otherPartyPK, setA, setB, nonce) {
  // Intersect the encrypted sets by comparing their elements
  const intersection = setA.filter((item) => setB.includes(item))
  // Decrypt the intersection using the private key of the other party
  const decryptedIntersection = intersection.map((item) =>
    nacl.box.open(toBuffer(item), nonce, otherPartyPK, secretKey)
  )
  // Return the decrypted intersection as an array of strings
  const dec = new TextDecoder()
  return decryptedIntersection.map((item) => dec.decode(item))
}

// Generate a fixed nonce
const nonce = nacl.randomBytes(nacl.box.nonceLength)
// Generate a new set of keys for Alice
const aliceKey = nacl.box.keyPair()
// Generate a new set of keys for Bob
const bobKey = nacl.box.keyPair()
// Alice's set
const aliceSet = ['(1,1)', '(2,2)', '(3,3)']
const aliceSetEncrypted = encrypted(
  aliceSet,
  aliceKey.secretKey,
  bobKey.publicKey,
  nonce
)
console.log('aliceSetEncrypted', aliceSetEncrypted)
// Bob's set
const bobSet = ['(3,1)', '(2,2)', '(1,3)']
const bobSetEncrypted = encrypted(
  bobSet,
  bobKey.secretKey,
  aliceKey.publicKey,
  nonce
)
console.log('bobSetEncrypted', bobSetEncrypted)
// Alice computes the intersection of her set with Bob's set
const intersection = PSI(
  aliceKey.secretKey,
  bobKey.publicKey,
  aliceSetEncrypted,
  bobSetEncrypted,
  nonce
)
console.log('the intersection', intersection)

There are several ways you can store context when dealing with callbacks in a smart contract system. One option is to use a mapping to store the context associated with a particular identifier. For example, you could have a mapping that maps player IDs to player data, and when you receive a callback with a player ID, you can look up the corresponding player data in the mapping.

Another option is to use a global variable to store the context. For example, you could have a global variable that stores the current player and amount, and when you receive a callback, you can use the global variable to determine the context.

A third option is to use an event to store the context. When you receive a callback, you can emit an event with the necessary context, and other contracts can listen for and react to the event as needed.

It's also worth considering whether it's necessary to use a callback at all. If the information you need is already available on the blockchain, you may be able to retrieve it directly rather than relying on a callback.

In general, the best approach will depend on the specific requirements of your use case and the trade-offs you are willing to make. It may be necessary to try out a few different approaches and see which one works best for your needs. src

Hypercube routing is a decentralized routing algorithm that can be used in smart contract systems to facilitate communication between nodes in a distributed network. It has nothing to do with storing context in the sense of storing state or data that is relevant to a particular function or operation.

In this case, the storage of context would not be the primary purpose of the hypercube routing algorithm, but it could be a useful side effect of its use.

In reply to this message
In the context of computer science and distributed systems, a hypercube is a graph structure that represents a network of nodes in which each node is connected to exactly k other nodes, where k is a fixed integer. The nodes in a hypercube are organized in a way that allows them to be addressed using k-dimensional coordinates.

For example, a 2-dimensional hypercube, also known as a square lattice, is a network of nodes in which each node is connected to four other nodes. In this case, the nodes can be identified using two coordinates (x and y). A 3-dimensional hypercube, also known as a cubic lattice, is a network of nodes in which each node is connected to eight other nodes. In this case, the nodes can be identified using three coordinates (x, y, and z).

Hypercubes are often used in distributed systems to represent a network of nodes that need to communicate with each other. They have some useful properties, such as a small diameter (the maximum number of steps it takes to get from one node to another), which makes them efficient for routing messages between nodes. The term "hypercube routing" refers to the use of a hypercube structure to facilitate communication between nodes in a distributed system.

42 üöß *OpenAI
In reply to this message
Hypercube routing is a decentralized routing algorithm that can be used in distributed systems to facilitate communication between nodes. It is based on the use of a hypercube structure, which is a network of nodes organized in a way that allows them to be addressed using k-dimensional coordinates, where k is a fixed integer.

To understand how hypercube routing works, it helps to consider an example. Suppose we have a 2-dimensional hypercube with four nodes, as shown below:

(0,1)---(1,1)
 |         |
(0,0)---(1,0)

In this example, each node is connected to four other nodes. We can represent the connections between nodes using an adjacency matrix:

  (0,1) (1,1)
(0,0)  0   1
  1     0   0
(1,0)

To route a message from node (0,0) to node (1,1), we can follow the following steps:

1. Find the coordinate of the destination node (1,1).
2. Find the most significant bit (MSB) in the coordinates of the destination node. In this case, the MSB is 1.
3. Look at the coordinates of the current node (0,0) and compare the value of the MSB to the value in the destination node. If the values are different, move to the node with the same value as the destination node for the MSB. If the values are the same, move to the next most significant bit.
4. Repeat this process until the destination node is reached.

Using this algorithm, we can route a message from any node to any other node in the hypercube in O(log n) steps, where n is the number of nodes in the hypercube. This makes hypercube routing an efficient way to communicate between nodes in a distributed system.

There are some variations on the basic hypercube routing algorithm, but the basic idea is the same: use the hypercube structure and the coordinates of the nodes to route messages between nodes in an efficient way.

The Open Network (TON) is a fast, secure, and scalable blockchain and network project that aims to host and provide a wide range of services and applications. It includes components such as a flexible multi-blockchain platform, a peer-to-peer network, a distributed file storage technology, and a payment network. The TON blockchain is designed to be able to process millions of transactions per second and support Turing-complete smart contracts. It includes features such as vertical self-healing mechanism, Instant Hypercube Routing, and support for micropayment channels and off-chain payment networks. The TON P2P network is used for accessing the TON blockchain and supporting arbitrary distributed services. TON Storage is a distributed file storage technology that allows users to store and access files on the TON network. The TON payment network includes payment channels and a payment channel network, also known as the "Lightning Network," which enables fast and cheap transactions. The TON platform is intended to be user-friendly and service provider-friendly.

The Telegram Open Network (TON) is a blockchain project developed by Telegram. It is intended to be fast, secure, and scalable, with the ability to host a wide range of services and applications. The TON Blockchain is designed to be able to process millions of transactions per second and support Turing-complete smart contracts. It includes features such as compatibility and validity conditions, message delivery guarantees, and a TON Virtual Machine (TVM) for executing smart contracts. The TON Blockchain is composed of a shardchain and a masterchain, which are connected by a BFT (Byzantine Fault Tolerant) protocol. The TON network includes a peer-to-peer network for propagating blocks, block candidates, and invalidity proofs. The TON platform also includes a distributed file storage technology called TON Storage and a payment network with payment channels and a payment channel network (also known as the "Lightning Network"). The TON blockchain uses elliptic curve cryptography for signing blocks and messages, which can also be accessed through TVM primitives. This document provides a detailed description of the TON Blockchain, including its block format, validity conditions, TVM invocation details, and cryptographic signatures.

TL-B is a binary serialization format used in the TON Blockchain. The Compact-u16 format is a way to encode unsigned 16-bit integers in TL-B.

To encode a Compact-u16 value in TL-B, you would first determine if the value can be represented in a single byte. If the value is less than 254, you can encode it as a single byte with the value itself as the byte's value. If the value is greater than or equal to 254, you would encode it as a two-byte sequence, with the first byte having a value of 254 and the second byte having the value of the 16-bit integer minus 254.

For example, to encode the value 300 in Compact-u16 format in TL-B, you would first determine that it is greater than or equal to 254, so you would use the two-byte encoding. The first byte would be 254 and the second byte would be 300 - 254 = 46. The TL-B representation of 300 in Compact-u16 format would be the bytes 254 and 46.

Compact-u16 Format —ç—Ç–æ —Å–∂–∞—Ç—ã–π —Ñ–æ—Ä–º–∞—Ç –¥–ª—è —Ö—Ä–∞–Ω–µ–Ω–∏—è —Ü–µ–ª—ã—Ö —á–∏—Å–µ–ª –≤ –¥–∏–∞–ø–∞–∑–æ–Ω–µ –æ—Ç 0 –¥–æ 65535 (2^16 - 1) –≤ TON Virtual Machine (TVM). –û–Ω —Ö—Ä–∞–Ω–∏—Ç—Å—è –≤ –≤–∏–¥–µ –æ–¥–Ω–æ–≥–æ –±–∞–π—Ç–∞ (–µ—Å–ª–∏ —á–∏—Å–ª–æ –º–µ–Ω—å—à–µ 128) –∏–ª–∏ —Ç—Ä–µ—Ö –±–∞–π—Ç (–µ—Å–ª–∏ —á–∏—Å–ª–æ –±–æ–ª—å—à–µ –∏–ª–∏ —Ä–∞–≤–Ω–æ 128). –í –ø–µ—Ä–≤–æ–º —Å–ª—É—á–∞–µ –∑–Ω–∞—á–µ–Ω–∏–µ —á–∏—Å–ª–∞ —Å–æ–≤–ø–∞–¥–∞–µ—Ç —Å–æ –∑–Ω–∞—á–µ–Ω–∏–µ–º –±–∞–π—Ç–∞, –≤–æ –≤—Ç–æ—Ä–æ–º —Å–ª—É—á–∞–µ –ø–µ—Ä–≤—ã–µ –¥–≤–∞ –±–∞–π—Ç–∞ —Ä–∞–≤–Ω—ã 128, –∞ —Ç—Ä–µ—Ç–∏–π –±–∞–π—Ç —Å–æ–¥–µ—Ä–∂–∏—Ç –∑–Ω–∞—á–µ–Ω–∏–µ —á–∏—Å–ª–∞ –º–∏–Ω—É—Å 128.

–ù–∞ TL-B —ç—Ç–æ—Ç —Ñ–æ—Ä–º–∞—Ç –º–æ–∂–µ—Ç –±—ã—Ç—å –≤—ã—Ä–∞–∂–µ–Ω —Å –ø–æ–º–æ—â—å—é —Å–ª–µ–¥—É—é—â–µ–π –∫–æ–Ω—Å—Ç—Ä—É–∫—Ü–∏–∏:

{
  value 16 u< if
    // —á–∏—Å–ª–æ –º–µ–Ω—å—à–µ 128, –∑–∞–ø–∏—Å—ã–≤–∞–µ–º –µ–≥–æ –∫–∞–∫ –µ—Å—Ç—å
    8 u,
  else
    // —á–∏—Å–ª–æ –±–æ–ª—å—à–µ –∏–ª–∏ —Ä–∞–≤–Ω–æ 128, –∑–∞–ø–∏—Å—ã–≤–∞–µ–º –ø–µ—Ä–≤—ã–µ –¥–≤–∞ –±–∞–π—Ç–∞ —Ä–∞–≤–Ω—ã–µ 128, –∞ —Ç—Ä–µ—Ç–∏–π –±–∞–π—Ç —Ä–∞–≤–Ω—ã–π value - 128
    128 16 u, value 128 - 16 u,
  then
}

A competition to hack the smart contracts of Token Bridge with a total prize pool of $50,000

The TON Foundation has completed its testing of the smart contracts for the long-awaited Token Bridge, which will allow users to transfer any token from the Ethereum network or BNB Smart Chain to the TON blockchain and vice versa.

Cross-chain bridges that handle a large number of tokens and coins are often the target of hacker attacks.

Therefore, the TON Foundation is hosting a hacking competition to crack the Token Bridge‚Äôs smart contracts, offering a total prize pool of $50,000. If participants can uncover vulnerabilities in the bridge‚Äôs Solidity or FunC smart contracts, they‚Äôll have a chance at receiving a reward from $200 all the way up to $25,000 (depending on the severity of the found vulnerabilities).

The competition will run until Dec. 30.

You can find all the competition‚Äôs details and rules here:
https://t.me/toncontests/101

‚ÄãIntroducing TON Connect 2.0

The TON community is launching TON Connect 2.0 today, an open-source standard allowing users to log in to internet services safely and without usernames or passwords.

This next iteration of TON Connect, launched in April, has received new yet important functions and was a genuine team effort from the community.

One of the key advantages TON Connect 2.0 has over its previous version is the ability to interact directly with decentralized applications based on TON. Users can now connect to mobile and desktop apps, Telegram bots, and other dApps in the interfaces of TON-based crypto wallets.

Connecting to apps happens in the wallet‚Äôs browser, and they‚Äôll be available to be used in the wallet ‚Äî without leaving the app.

TON Connect 2.0 allows users to sign in to online platforms with a single click, granting access to all of Web3‚Äôs use cases.

Tonkeeper already supports TON Connect 2.0‚Äôs standard, but other wallets have been working on implementing it.

We encourage developers and businesses to integrate TON Connect 2.0 into their products.

You can find TON Connect 2.0‚Äôs SDK documentation, helpful integration guides, and a description of the updated standard here:
https://tonkeeper.gitbook.io/ton-connect-2.0-guide-for-sdk

TON Connect 2.0 has all the tools needed to become a network-wide standard.
